  <script>
    window.AUTH_HOST = 'https://myaccount.nytimes.com';
    window.SWG_PUBLICATION_ID = 'nytimes.com';
    window.swgUserInfoXhrObject.checkSwgResponse = false;
    function swgAbort(reason) {
  window.console.log('aborted ' + reason);
  window.swgUserInfoXhrObject.checkSwgResponse = {};
  var event = new Event('aborted');
  window.swgUserInfoXhrObject.dispatchEvent(event);
  window.swgUserInfoXhrObject.abort();
  return false;
};
    function checkSwgOptOut() {
  if (!window.localStorage) return false;
  var opt = window.localStorage.getItem('nyt-swgOptOut');
  if (!opt) return false;

  var expiry = parseInt(opt);
  var today = new Date().getTime();
  var twentyFourHours = 1000 * 60 * 60 * 24;
  var difference = (today - expiry) / twentyFourHours;
  if (difference < 1) {
    return true;
  } else {
    window.localStorage.removeItem('nyt-swgOptOut');
  }
  return false;
};
    function swgDeferredAccount(swg, entitlements) {
  swg.completeDeferredAccountCreation({
    entitlements: entitlements,
    consent: false
  }).then(function (res) {
    // LIRE service that creates NYT account
    var url = window.AUTH_HOST + '/svc/account/auth/v1/swg-dal-web';
    var data = res.purchaseData.raw.data ? res.purchaseData.raw.data : res.purchaseData.raw;
    var subscriptionToken = JSON.parse(data);
    var payload = {
      package_name: subscriptionToken.packageName,
      product_id: subscriptionToken.productId,
      purchase_token: subscriptionToken.purchaseToken,
      google_id_token: res.userData.idToken,
      google_user_email: res.userData.email,
      google_user_id: res.userData.id,
      google_user_name: res.userData.name
    };
    xhrDeferred = new XMLHttpRequest();
    xhrDeferred.withCredentials = true;
    xhrDeferred.open('POST', url, true);
    xhrDeferred.setRequestHeader('Content-Type', 'application/json');
    xhrDeferred.send(JSON.stringify(payload));
    xhrDeferred.onload = function () {
      if (xhrDeferred.status === 200) {
        swgDataLayer({
          name: 'swg',
          context: 'Deferred',
          label: 'Seamless Signin',
          region: 'swg-modal'
        });
        res.complete().then(function () {
          window.location.reload(true);
        });
      } else {
        res.complete();
        window.location = encodeURI(window.AUTH_HOST + '/get-started/swg-link?redirect=' + window.location.href);
      }
    };
  }).catch(err => {
    if (!window.localStorage) return false;
    if (window.localStorage.getItem('nyt-swgOptOut')) return false;
    window.localStorage.setItem('nyt-swgOptOut', new Date().getTime());
  });
  return true;
};
    function swgDataLayer(module) {
  if (!window.dataLayer) return false;
  (window.dataLayer = window.dataLayer || []).push({
    event: 'impression',
    module: module
  });
  return true;
};
    function loginWithGoogle() {
  // this cannot be done in the server
  if (typeof window === 'undefined') return swgAbort('no window');
  // check if NYT-S cookie is already set
  if (document.cookie.indexOf('NYT-S') !== -1) return swgAbort('NYT-S');
  // check if they deferred opt out
  if (checkSwgOptOut() === true) return swgAbort('opt-out');
  // check if the swg object is set
  if (!window.SWG) return swgAbort('swg');
  // check swg entitlenments
  (window.SWG = window.SWG || []).push(swg => {
    swg.init(window.SWG_PUBLICATION_ID);
    swg.getEntitlements().then(ent => {
      if (typeof ent !== 'undefined' && ent.raw) {
        var payload = {
          entitlements_token: ent.raw
        };
        window.swgUserInfoXhrObject.withCredentials = true;
        window.swgUserInfoXhrObject.open('POST', window.AUTH_HOST + '/svc/account/auth/v1/login-swg-web', true);
        window.swgUserInfoXhrObject.setRequestHeader('Content-Type', 'application/json');
        window.swgUserInfoXhrObject.send(JSON.stringify(payload));
        window.swgUserInfoXhrObject.onload = function () {
          switch (window.swgUserInfoXhrObject.status) {
            case 200:
              // this is handled by network/getUser observerable
              swgDataLayer({
                name: 'swg',
                context: 'Seamless',
                label: 'Seamless Signin',
                region: 'login'
              });
              window.location.reload(true);
              return true;
            case 412:
              // account deferred creation
              swgAbort('412');
              return swgDeferredAccount(swg, ent);
            default:
              // don't do anything fail silently
              return swgAbort(window.swgUserInfoXhrObject.status);
          };
        };
      } else {
        return swgAbort('entitlements 1');
      }
    }).catch(() => {
      // don't do anything fail silently
      return swgAbort('entitlements 2');
    });
  });
};
    (function() {
      if (document.cookie.indexOf('NYT-S') === -1) {
        var script = document.createElement('script');
        script.src = 'https://news.google.com/swg/js/v1/swg.js';
        script.setAttribute('subscriptions-control', 'manual');
        script.setAttribute('async', true);
        script.onload = function () {
          loginWithGoogle();
        }
        script.onerror = function () {
          swgAbort('swg');
        }
        document.getElementById('swg-script').appendChild(script);
      }
    })();
  </script>

Mills/The New York Times</span></span></span></figcaption></figure></div><div class="css-umkxhb"><h1 class="css-11nnyz5 ejekc6u0" itemProp="headline" id="link-58ba66ce"><span>For Trump, ‘a War Every Day,’ Waged Increasingly Alone</span></h1><p class="css-97enoo ewc5vgb0">At the midpoint of his term, the president has grown more sure of his own judgment and more isolated from anyone else’s than at any point since he took office.</p></div></header><div class="css-nwzfg5"><p class="css-14xmeol"><span class="css-1f9pvn2 politics">President Trump has grown increasingly suspicious of many of the people around him, convinced that they are fools.</span><span itemProp="copyrightHolder" class="emkp2hg2 css-1nwzsjy e18m0s9i0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1afaoz0">Credit</span><span><span class="css-1dv1kvn">Credit</span><span>Doug Mills/The New York Times</span></span></span></p></div><div id="sponsor-wrapper" class="css-1hyfx7x"><div id="sponsor-slug" class="css-19vbshk"><p>Supported 3.215.406 8.107.406 12.66 1.653 9.133 4.29 7.517 8.978 7.517v3.2l6.428-5.358z" fill="#000"></path></svg></button></div></li><li class="css-ohqqbz"></li><li class="css-1em32ry"><span class=""><i class="OpenCommentsButton-icon--2fdWB"><span class="OpenCommentsButton-text--2uwLE"></span></i></span></li></ul></div></div></li></ul></div></div></div><section name="articleBody" itemProp="articleBody" class="css-1572rug"><div class="css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-4w7y5l"><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">WASHINGTON — When President Trump grows frustrated with advisers during meetings, which is not an uncommon occurrence, he sits back in his chair, crosses his arms and scowls. Often he erupts. “Freaking idiots!” he calls his aides. Except he uses a more pungent word than “freaking.”</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">For two years, Mr. Trump has waged war against his own government, convinced that people around him are fools. Angry that they resist his wishes, uninterested in the details of their briefings, he becomes especially agitated when they tell him he does not have the power to do what he wants, which makes him suspicious that they are secretly undermining him.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Now, the president who once declared that <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2016/07/22/us/politics/trump-transcript-rnc-address.html?module=inline" title="">“I alone can fix”</a> the system increasingly stands alone in a system that seems as broken as ever. The swirl of recent days — a <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/21/us/politics/trump-shutdown-border-wall.html?action=click&amp;module=inline&amp;pgtype=Homepage" title="">government shutdown</a>, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/18/us/politics/michael-flynn-sentencing.html?module=inline" title="">spiraling scandals</a>, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/21/business/nasdaq-stocks-bear-market.html?action=click&amp;module=inline&amp;pgtype=Homepage" title="">tumbling stock markets</a>, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/19/us/politics/trump-syria-turkey-troop-withdrawal.html?module=inline" title="">abrupt troop withdrawals</a> and the <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/20/us/politics/jim-mattis-defense-secretary-trump.html?module=inline" title="">resignation of his alienated defense secretary</a> — has left the impression of a presidency at risk of spinning out of control.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">[<a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/22/us/politics/trump-2018.html?module=inline" title=""><em class="css-2fg4z9 ehxkw330">2018 in Review: The Highs and Lows of Trump’s Year</em></a>]</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">At the midpoint of his term, Mr. Trump has grown more sure of his own judgment and more cut off from anyone else’s than at any point since taking office. He spends ever more time in front of a television, often retreating to his residence out of concern that he is being watched too closely. As he sheds advisers at a head-spinning rate, he reaches out to old associates, complaining that few of the people around him were there at the beginning.</p></div><aside class="css-1m2ozyi"><span></span></aside></div><div class="css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-4w7y5l"><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Mr. Trump is said by advisers to be consumed by the multiplying investigations that have taken down his <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/12/nyregion/michael-cohen-sentence-trump.html?module=inline" title="">personal lawyer</a>, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/07/us/politics/manafort-special-counsel-lies.html?module=inline" title="">campaign chairman</a>, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/18/us/politics/michael-flynn-sentencing.html?module=inline" title="">national security adviser</a> and <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/18/nyregion/ny-ag-underwood-trump-foundation.html?module=inline" title="">family foundation</a>. He rails against enemies, who often were once friends, nursing a deep sense of betrayal and grievance as they turn on him.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">“Can you believe this?” he has said as he scanned the torrent of headlines. “I’m doing great, but it’s a war every day.”</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">“Why is it like this?” he has asked aides, with no acknowledgment that he might have played a role. The aides, many of whom believe he has been treated unfairly by the news media, have replied that journalists are angry that he won and proved them wrong. He nods in agreement at such explanations.</p></div><aside class="css-1m2ozyi"><span></span></aside></div><div data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper" class="css-79elbk ehw59r11"><div class="css-z3e15g" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper-hidden"></div><div data-testid="photoviewer-children" class="css-1a48zt4 ehw59r111"><figure class="css-1ef8w8q e1a8i6eb0" aria-label="media" role="group" itemProp="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemID="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/12/23/us/23dc-trump-shutdown-2/merlin_148386687_953a8769-6e43-4554-9ef2-632b2f365b4e-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemType="http://schema.org/ImageObject"><div class="css-1xdhyk6 e1vv25i80"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1afaoz0">Image</span><img alt="" class="css-1m50asq" src="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/12/23/us/23dc-trump-shutdown-2/merlin_148386687_953a8769-6e43-4554-9ef2-632b2f365b4e-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale" srcSet="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/12/23/us/23dc-trump-shutdown-2/merlin_148386687_953a8769-6e43-4554-9ef2-632b2f365b4e-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 600w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/12/23/us/23dc-trump-shutdown-2/merlin_148386687_953a8769-6e43-4554-9ef2-632b2f365b4e-jumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 1024w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/12/23/us/23dc-trump-shutdown-2/merlin_148386687_953a8769-6e43-4554-9ef2-632b2f365b4e-superJumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 2048w" sizes="((min-width: 600px) and (max-width: 1004px)) 84vw, (min-width: 1005px) 80vw, 100vw" itemProp="url" itemID="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/12/23/us/23dc-trump-shutdown-2/merlin_148386687_953a8769-6e43-4554-9ef2-632b2f365b4e-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale"/></div><figcaption itemProp="caption description" class="css-18crmh6 e3zkro30"><span class="css-8i9d0s e1olku6u0">The swirl of recent days, including the government shutdown, has left the impression of a presidency at risk of spinning out of control.</span><span itemProp="copyrightHolder" class="css-vuqh7u e18m0s9i0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1afaoz0">Credit</span><span>Erin Schaff for The New York Times</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-4w7y5l"><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">As the president vents, he constantly rattles off what he sees as underappreciated accomplishments. “Look what I did for Mexico and Canada,” he has told allies. “Look what’s happened with terrorism.”</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">The portrait that emerges from interviews with about 30 current and former administration officials, personal friends, political allies, lawmakers and congressional aides suggests a president who revels in sharp swings in direction, feels free to disregard historic allies and presides over near constant turmoil within his own team as he follows his own instincts.</p></div><aside class="css-1m2ozyi"><span></span></aside></div><div class="css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-4w7y5l"><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">White House officials did not respond to requests for comment. But as the president struggles to find a way forward, the path is about to become much more hazardous. As tumultuous as events have been so far, Mr. Trump’s first two years may ultimately look calm compared to what lies ahead.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">In less than two weeks, Representative Nancy Pelosi of California will take the speaker’s gavel held until now by Representative Paul D. Ryan of Wisconsin, and subpoena-wielding House Democrats will be empowered to investigate Mr. Trump’s family, business, campaign and administration. At some point after that, he will face the results of whatever Robert S. Mueller III, the special counsel, finds about campaign ties to Russia and obstruction of justice.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">At some point after that, Ms. Pelosi may come under enormous pressure from her liberal base to open an impeachment inquiry, and many Republicans anticipate a battle over whether Mr. Trump committed <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/10/13/sunday-review/is-trump-on-a-collision-course-with-impeachment.html?module=inline" title="">high crimes and misdemeanors</a> even if they hold enough votes in the Senate to block removal. Amid all that is a rising budget deficit that will shrink Mr. Trump’s domestic options and signs of a possible economic downturn that would undercut his most potent bragging point.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">“Nothing he’s going to face in the next two years is going to be like the challenges of the previous two years,” said Michael Steel, a longtime adviser to Republicans like Mr. Ryan and former Speaker John A. Boehner. “Given the staff turnover and the increasing feeling that the president is encircled or cornered by legal and political enemies, it’s entirely possible it gets worse, not better.”</p></div><aside class="css-1m2ozyi"><span></span></aside></div><div style="max-width:100%;margin:0 auto" class=""><div data-id="100000006277188" data-slug="23dc-trump-2" class="full css-17w5iin"><iframe src="https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2018/admin/100000006277188.embedded.html?" class="css-sqdgy8" frameBorder="0" scrolling="no" width="100%"></iframe></div></div><div class="css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-4w7y5l"><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0"></p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Yet even with a 38 percent approval rating <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://news.gallup.com/poll/203207/trump-job-approval-weekly.aspx" title="" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">in Gallup polling</a>, Mr. Trump has dominated the national conversation as no other modern president has, and his base thrills at his fights with the establishment, seeing him as a warrior against self-satisfied elites who look down on many Americans. Determined to maintain that base, he has insisted — despite the seemingly long odds — on his pledge of a border wall, aware that abandoning his signature campaign promise would make him less authentic, the quality that his voters often cite as his appeal.</p></div><aside class="css-1m2ozyi"><span></span></aside></div><div data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper" class="css-79elbk ehw59r11"><div class="css-z3e15g" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper-hidden"></div><div data-testid="photoviewer-children" class="css-1a48zt4 ehw59r111"><figure class="css-1ef8w8q e1a8i6eb0" aria-label="media" role="group" itemProp="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemID="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/12/23/us/23dc-trump-border-2/merlin_146119029_77b5283b-0b20-4852-9d1b-a05fe5af8dff-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemType="http://schema.org/ImageObject"><div class="css-1xdhyk6 e1vv25i80"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1afaoz0">Image</span><div class="css-zjzyr8"><div data-testid="lazyimage-container" style="height:257.77777777777777px"></div></div></div><figcaption itemProp="caption description" class="css-18crmh6 e3zkro30"><span class="css-8i9d0s e1olku6u0">Mr. Trump has struggled with fellow Republicans lately too, including when he seemed to retreat on his demand for border wall funding.</span><span itemProp="copyrightHolder" class="css-vuqh7u e18m0s9i0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1afaoz0">Credit</span><span>Todd Heisler/The New York Times</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-4w7y5l"><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">As a result, a partisan war may be just what he wants. He has privately told associates that he is glad Democrats won the House in last month’s midterm elections, saying he thinks that guarantees his re-election because they will serve as a useful antagonist. That may be bravado, but history provides some support. Bill Clinton and Barack Obama, his Democratic predecessors, both endured even bigger midterm setbacks and went on to win re-election.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">“It’s absolutely fair to say that it’s better to have Nancy Pelosi as a foil than Paul Ryan as a foil,” said Marc Short, the president’s former legislative affairs director. “It’s better for the party and it’s better for unity.” He added, “The reality is the Democrats could overplay their hand.”</p></div><aside class="css-1m2ozyi"><span></span></aside></div><div class="css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-4w7y5l"><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Both sides gamely talk about possible cooperation on issues like rebuilding the nation’s tattered network of roads, bridges and other infrastructure. “The opportunity in the era of divided government is to work with both sides to get something done for the country,” said Representative Josh Gottheimer, Democrat from New Jersey and co-chairman of the bipartisan Problem Solvers Caucus.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Recent days showed the possibilities when <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/18/us/politics/senate-criminal-justice-bill.html?module=inline" title="">Congress overhauled the criminal justice system</a>, the most significant bipartisan domestic legislation of Mr. Trump’s tenure. Mr. Trump has hopes of winning bipartisan support for his new trade deal with Mexico and Canada. But one congressional Democrat said the party has gone from thinking it could make deals with Mr. Trump to believing he must be stopped at all costs because he is so dangerous.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0"></p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Mr. Trump has struggled with fellow Republicans lately, too. They objected loudly to his decisions to draw down troops from Syria and Afghanistan and <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/13/us/politics/yemen-saudi-war-pompeo-mattis.html?module=inline" title="">pushed through a Senate resolution</a> essentially rebuking his handling of Saudi Arabia after the assassination of the dissident Jamal Khashoggi. The departure of Defense Secretary Jim Mattis, who protested the troop pullouts and Mr. Trump’s cavalier approach to America’s alliances, sent shudders through Republican ranks.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">House conservatives, meanwhile, revolted against Mr. Trump when he seemed to retreat on his demand for border wall funding to avert a government shutdown. At the same time, Senate Republicans, who had voted unanimously to keep the government open without the wall money Mr. Trump had demanded, were angry when he reversed course and refused to sign such a measure.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Such conflict comes with a cost. Mr. Trump has a way of stepping on his own successes. The border wall fight overshadowed his signing of the criminal justice overhaul. The abrupt way he decided to withdraw from Syria overshadowed the military victories against the Islamic State. Mr. Trump’s focus during the midterm campaign on a caravan of migrants overshadowed the positive economic story he had to tell before the latest stock market gyrations.</p></div><aside class="css-1m2ozyi"><span></span></aside></div><div data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper" class="css-79elbk ehw59r11"><div class="css-z3e15g" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper-hidden"></div><div data-testid="photoviewer-children" class="css-1a48zt4 ehw59r111"><figure class="css-1ef8w8q e1a8i6eb0" aria-label="media" role="group" itemProp="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemID="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/12/23/us/23dc-trump-syria/merlin_148355769_ada9c2d1-1d25-4666-a13f-de2151aef353-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemType="http://schema.org/ImageObject"><div class="css-1xdhyk6 e1vv25i80"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1afaoz0">Image</span><div class="css-zjzyr8"><div data-testid="lazyimage-container" style="height:257.77777777777777px"></div></div></div><figcaption itemProp="caption description" class="css-18crmh6 e3zkro30"><span class="css-8i9d0s e1olku6u0">Republicans also objected loudly to Mr. Trump’s decision to draw down troops from Syria.</span><span itemProp="copyrightHolder" class="css-vuqh7u e18m0s9i0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1afaoz0">Credit</span><span>Mauricio Lima for The New York Times</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-4w7y5l"><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">“When he’s talking about the economy, he’s gotten a much more positive reaction,” said David Winston, a Republican pollster. “Obviously on other topics he hasn’t been able to get it to the point where he’s able to create an overall job approval. The question is, do you address weaknesses or do you play to your strengths?”</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Always impulsive, the president increasingly believes he does not need advisers, according to people close to him. He is on his third chief of staff, third national security adviser, sixth communications director, second secretary of state, second attorney general and soon his second defense secretary. Turnover at the top has <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.brookings.edu/research/tracking-turnover-in-the-trump-administration/" title="" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">reached 65 percent</a>, according to the Brookings Institution.</p></div><aside class="css-1m2ozyi"><span></span></aside></div><div class="css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-4w7y5l"><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Some left in a cloud of corruption allegations, including his health and human services secretary, his Environmental Protection Agency chief and, most recently, his interior secretary. Others left after clashing with Mr. Trump. Mr. Mattis was the last of the so-called axis of adults seen by some as tempering a volatile president, following the ouster of Rex W. Tillerson as secretary of state, H. R. McMaster as national security adviser and John F. Kelly as chief of staff.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">“Rex Tillerson and Jim Mattis are two of the finest people ever to serve in government,” said Steve Goldstein, who was under secretary of state until he was fired along with Mr. Tillerson. “They were very close and worked hard at trying to do what was best for the country, and sometimes that meant being brutally honest with folks at the White House.”</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">But Fred Fleitz, who worked for nearly six months this year as chief of staff for John R. Bolton, the current national security adviser, said the new team is more cohesive and better suited to Mr. Trump than one constantly undermining him.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">“He came in as the ultimate outsider and he brought in some unorthodox policies that worked and he tried some things that didn’t work, and one of the things that didn’t work was bringing in some staffers who didn’t work, like McMaster and Tillerson,” Mr. Fleitz said.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0"></p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0"><a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/07/us/politics/trump-tillerson.html?module=inline" title="">In a recent public talk</a>, Mr. Tillerson said out loud what others say in private, that Mr. Trump often pushes for actions that exceed his authority and does not like it when told he cannot do something. He bristles at constraints and expresses envy of autocrats like President Xi Jinping of China who do not have to deal with independent power centers like the Federal Reserve or the courts.</p></div><aside class="css-1m2ozyi"><span></span></aside></div><div data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper" class="css-79elbk ehw59r11"><div class="css-z3e15g" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper-hidden"></div><div data-testid="photoviewer-children" class="css-1a48zt4 ehw59r111"><figure class="css-1ef8w8q e1a8i6eb0" aria-label="media" role="group" itemProp="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemID="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/12/23/us/23dc-trump-cohen/merlin_148057056_3567923e-2ead-4ab3-a375-01eca30a0889-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemType="http://schema.org/ImageObject"><div class="css-1xdhyk6 e1vv25i80"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1afaoz0">Image</span><div class="css-zjzyr8"><div data-testid="lazyimage-container" style="height:257.77777777777777px"></div></div></div><figcaption itemProp="caption description" class="css-18crmh6 e3zkro30"><span class="css-8i9d0s e1olku6u0">Mr. Trump was infuriated by his former personal lawyer, Michael D. Cohen, who pleaded guilty to organizing an <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/12/nyregion/michael-cohen-sentence-trump.html">illegal scheme to pay hush money</a> to women to keep quiet about alleged affairs with Mr. Trump before the 2016 election, a scheme he said Mr. Trump had directed.</span><span itemProp="copyrightHolder" class="css-vuqh7u e18m0s9i0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1afaoz0">Credit</span><span>Stephanie Keith for The New York Times</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-4w7y5l"><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">In recent days, Mr. Trump has asked aides whether he can fire Jerome H. Powell, the Fed chairman he appointed, telling advisers that Mr. Powell will “turn me into Hoover,” a reference to the Great Depression-era president. During a meeting with German car executives this month, Mr. Trump threatened to impose higher fuel efficiency standards on their imported cars than required on American vehicles even though aides told him he could not do that.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">And he can be hard on his staff. He regularly curses at them, some say. Even his humor can be abrasive. When Larry Kudlow, his economics adviser, returned after <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/06/11/business/larry-kudlow-heart-attack.html?module=inline" title="">a heart attack</a> this year, the president ribbed him in front of aides. “Larry, you’re here six weeks and you had a heart attack?” Others laughed uncomfortably.</p></div><aside class="css-1m2ozyi"><span></span></aside></div><div class="css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-4w7y5l"><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">More recently, the president has told associates he feels “totally and completely abandoned,” as one put it, complaining that no one is on his side and that many around him have ulterior motives. That extends even to his son-in-law, Jared Kushner, who was <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/14/us/politics/jared-kushner-criminal-justice-bill.html?module=inline" title="">credited for helping push through</a> the criminal justice bill, praise that Mr. Trump took note of.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Longtime associates said Mr. Trump’s relationship with his children has grown more removed and that he feels he does not have a friend in the White House. He disagrees with Mr. Kushner and Ivanka Trump much of the time, but cannot bring himself to tell them no, leaving that instead to Mr. Kelly, according to former aides. That made Mr. Kelly the heavy, they said, and therefore the target of their ire until he was <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/08/us/politics/john-kelly-chief-staff-trump.html?module=inline" title="">finally forced out.</a></p></div><aside class="css-1m2ozyi"><span></span></aside></div><div data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper" class="css-79elbk ehw59r11"><div class="css-z3e15g" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper-hidden"></div><div data-testid="photoviewer-children" class="css-1a48zt4 ehw59r111"><figure class="css-1lghxe1 e1a8i6eb0" aria-label="media" role="group" itemProp="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemID="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/12/23/us/23dc-trump-kelly-2/merlin_148011354_10557601-db4a-46d2-94db-c452eba0f80c-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemType="http://schema.org/ImageObject"><div class="css-1xdhyk6 e1vv25i80"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1afaoz0">Image</span><div class="css-zjzyr8"><div data-testid="lazyimage-container" style="height:257.77777777777777px"></div></div></div><figcaption itemProp="caption description" class="css-1l44abu e3zkro30"><span class="css-8i9d0s e1olku6u0">John F. Kelly at the White House with Jared Kushner earlier this month. When Mr. Trump disagreed with Mr. Kushner, he would leave it to Mr. Kelly to handle.</span><span itemProp="copyrightHolder" class="css-vuqh7u e18m0s9i0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1afaoz0">Credit</span><span>Doug Mills/The New York Times</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-4w7y5l"><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Mr. Trump has spent far less time lately with older friends. The sense of isolation was on display at this month’s holiday parties when he appeared for a few minutes, took a few perfunctory photographs with preselected guests and then disappeared back upstairs rather than mingle. He spent part of this preholiday shutdown weekend alone until Melania could return from Florida.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Mr. Trump still views the presidency through the lens of a television showman. He told his staff that he wanted a fireworks display over Mount Rushmore. Before signing the farm bill, he <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://twitter.com/realDonaldTrump/status/1075846949427908608?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw%7Ctwcamp%5Etweetembed%7Ctwterm%5E1075846949427908608&amp;ref_url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.latimes.com%2Fentertainment%2Fla-et-st-megan-mullally-trump-tweet-20181221-story.html" title="" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">posted a goofy video</a> of himself at the 2005 Emmy Awards dressed in overalls and a straw hat, holding a pitchfork and singing the theme song to the old television show “Green Acres.”</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">For election night in November, he insisted on throwing a lavish party in the East Room and originally wanted aides to be on display for his guests, a simulation of officials gathering election return information like aides did at Trump Tower in 2016. When White House officials warned that a party would look discordant given the likelihood of losing the House, he insisted on going forward anyway.</p></div><aside class="css-1m2ozyi"><span></span></aside></div><div data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper" class="css-79elbk ehw59r11"><div class="css-z3e15g" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper-hidden"></div><div data-testid="photoviewer-children" class="css-1a48zt4 ehw59r111"><figure class="css-1ef8w8q e1a8i6eb0" aria-label="media" role="group" itemProp="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemID="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/12/23/us/23dc-trump-jp/merlin_138781122_52c491d2-35eb-41cb-b56c-484e8c9db462-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemType="http://schema.org/ImageObject"><div class="css-1xdhyk6 e1vv25i80"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1afaoz0">Image</span><div class="css-zjzyr8"><div data-testid="lazyimage-container" style="height:257.77777777777777px"></div></div></div><figcaption itemProp="caption description" class="css-18crmh6 e3zkro30"><span class="css-8i9d0s e1olku6u0">Mr. Trump is said by advisers to be consumed by the multiplying investigations that have taken down his personal lawyer, campaign chairman, national security adviser and family foundation.</span><span itemProp="copyrightHolder" class="css-vuqh7u e18m0s9i0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1afaoz0">Credit</span><span>Tom Brenner/The New York Times</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-4w7y5l"><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">By all accounts, Mr. Trump’s consumption of cable television has actually increased in recent months as his first scheduled meetings of the day have slid back from the 9 or 9:30 a.m. set by Reince Priebus, his first chief of staff, to roughly 11 many mornings. During “executive time,” Mr. Trump watches television in the residence for hours, reacting to what he sees on Fox News. While in the West Wing, he leaves it on during most meetings in the dining room off the Oval Office, one ear attuned to what is being said.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0"></p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Of late, allies concede, the news has been particularly grim. He was infuriated by his former personal lawyer, Michael D. Cohen, who pleaded guilty to organizing an <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/12/nyregion/michael-cohen-sentence-trump.html?module=inline" title="">illegal scheme to pay hush money</a> to women to keep quiet about alleged affairs with Mr. Trump before the 2016 election, a scheme he said Mr. Trump had directed.</p></div><aside class="css-1m2ozyi"><span></span></aside></div><div class="css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-4w7y5l"><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Still, for all the reports of a fuming president alarmed at possible impeachment, Mr. Trump rarely expresses such specific anxiety out loud, associates said. Instead he expresses frustration, anger, mania — all of which aides read like tea leaves to discern what lies beneath.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">“It will be a challenge not to be consumed by it,” Mr. Short said. “It would only be human when it’s the coverage leading the news every day to be distracted, but it will be important to have the internal discipline not to be.”</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">No one outside of Mr. Mueller’s office, of course, knows for sure what he will report, but so far he and other prosecutors have drawn a devastating picture of a president surrounded by people who have lied to the authorities, cheated on their taxes, skirted campaign finance laws and secretly worked for foreign interests. The question is what Mr. Mueller will say about Mr. Trump.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">“Does he create a story that the man never put the presidency first?” asked Doris Kearns Goodwin, whose newest book, “<a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/09/14/books/review/doris-kearns-goodwin-leadership.html?module=inline" title="">Leadership: In Turbulent Times</a>,” chronicles four presidents. “There has to be a narrative. The individual things may not hit the people who support him, but if there’s an overall narrative, people may understand.”</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">Mr. Trump has not helped himself with decisions that opponents use to draw a narrative of a president unusually deferential to Russia, including his withdrawal of troops in Syria, which drew the public approval of <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/20/world/europe/putin-trump-syria.html?module=inline" title=""> President Vladimir V. Putin</a>.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">“Mueller will decide whether there’s collusion with the Russians on the election,” said Mayor Rahm Emanuel of Chicago, a former White House chief of staff to Mr. Obama. “But we can now say there’s collusion with the Russians on foreign policy, and it’s not to America’s benefit.”</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">More recently, Mr. Trump has taken to spending time reminiscing about the happier days of his candidacy and his 2016 victory. He spent the fall showing different groups of visitors what he calls his love letters from North Korea’s iron-fisted dictator, Kim Jong-un, expressing admiration for Mr. Trump. And he still takes joy in aspects of the job, primarily those that demonstrate power. “The roads closed for me!” he declared to friends this year after a motorcade ride.</p></div><aside class="css-1m2ozyi"><span></span></aside></div><div class="css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-4w7y5l"><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">But those highs have been hard to recapture. The days are filled with conflict, much of it of his own making. More advisers are heading for the door. The divisions are widening, not closing. If it is a “war every day,” there are no signs of peace.</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0">“What I’m trying to figure out is where does it end,” Mr. Goldstein said. “The language gets coarser on all sides. The respect for the office of the presidency seems less to me than it was. How do we move people back? Or are we in the new reality?”</p></div><aside class="css-1m2ozyi"><span></span></aside></div></section><div class="bottom-of-article"><div class="css-e25ngt"></div><div class="css-1gybuqn"> <p><em>Follow Peter Baker and Maggie Haberman on Twitter: <a href="https://twitter.com/peterbakernyt">@peterbakernyt</a> and <a href="https://twitter.com/maggienyt">@maggienyt</a>.</em></p></div><div
